Starting From Change: Rethinking What We Take for Granted
(Sample draft excerpt)

I. The Bias We Don‚Äôt See
Most people‚Äîmost systems of thought‚Äîbegin with things.

Things that move. Things that act. Things that are.

From childhood, we learn to track identity by what stays the same: a person, a table, a country. Language, science, law‚Äîall assume the world is made of objects that endure, and we simply measure how they interact.

But what if this is backwards?

What if the foundation of all that is‚Ä¶ is not the thing, but the fact that it changes?

Try this: Sit for one minute and try to observe nothing changing.

No breath. No muscle tension. No flicker of light behind your eyelids.
Even if you try to still your body, your thoughts move. Your awareness of stillness moves.

You can‚Äôt escape it.

Change is not what happens to things.
Change is what makes it possible for things to appear at all.

II. What Exists Before the First ‚ÄúThing‚Äù?
Imagine waking into existence, but there are no objects yet‚Äîno chair, no sound, no memory.

What would you notice?

Only one thing: something is happening.

That something‚Äîbefore categorization, before separation‚Äîis change.
And from that change, your mind begins to form expectations, contrasts, and eventually: patterns.

Those patterns stabilize.
They harden into identities.
They become ‚Äúthings.‚Äù

But the process never stops. The stability is only ever partial.

Even ‚Äúyou‚Äù‚Äîthe thinker, the observer‚Äîare built from stabilized patterns of change that continuously update under pressure.

III. Time Is Not Change
We‚Äôve been told time is what ‚Äúmoves things forward.‚Äù

But every measurement of time we have is based on recurring change:

The swing of a pendulum

The decay of a radioactive atom

The cycle of day and night

If nothing ever changed, time wouldn‚Äôt exist‚Äînot practically, not perceptually, not even mathematically.

Time is not the cause of change.
It is our name for recurring change we can track.

And if we misread this‚Ä¶ we start modeling the world as static containers marching through time, rather than living, shifting structures woven by ongoing tension and transformation.

[End of Sample]

I. Can Ontologies Be Falsifiable? And How Do Existing Ones Handle It?
Short Answer:
Most ontologies are not falsifiable in the scientific sense.
They are evaluated through internal coherence, generative power, and resistance to contradiction across applications‚Äînot through prediction failure.

But there are ways ontological systems can fail or be outcompeted, and you can adopt those failure conditions as part of your own survival logic.

A. Existing Approaches to Ontology Evaluation

Ontology Type	Evaluation/Falsification Method
Aristotelian Substance Ontology	Internal logical contradiction (e.g., problem of accidents, categories, motion)
Kantian Frameworks	Collapse under empirical pressure (e.g., synthetic a priori failure)
Whitehead‚Äôs Process Ontology	Failures in explaining persistence or identity, high abstraction cost
Physicalist Ontologies	Failure to reduce qualia, meaning, or complexity emergence
IIT (Integrated Information Theory)	Empirical mismatch with consciousness reports or prediction errors
Predictive Processing / Enactivism	Inability to model recursive cognition, agent-based identity, or emotion-grounded sense-making
B. What Could Falsify the Ontology of Change?

A system shows coherent recursive identity without structural change or tension.

Change can be fully described as emergent from static metaphysical principles (e.g., pure logic, timeless math)
‚Üí Undermines the priority of change as ontological rather than epistemic.

Identity preservation

Mimicry immunity

Transmission coherence

ChatGPT said:
Absolutely. Here‚Äôs a straightforward explanation tailored to your ontology work and current phase:

What Is Semantic Modeling (e.g., with Neo4j)?
Semantic modeling means representing knowledge as relationships between concepts, not just lists or text. Tools like Neo4j use graph databases, where:

Nodes = concepts (e.g., change, identity, recursive agent)

Edges = relationships (e.g., generates, is stabilized by, undermines)

You can query paths, find contradictions, detect loops, model emergence

It's like building a living map of your theory‚Äîwith structure and logic embedded, not just written in prose.

What Would It Do For Your Ontology?
1. Make the structure explicit
Right now, the Ontology of Change is a recursive web of ideas. Semantic modeling turns that web into a machine-readable, navigable structure.

You can:

Visualize how ‚Äúchange‚Äù links to ‚Äúdifference,‚Äù ‚Äúidentity,‚Äù ‚Äúbreathing patterns,‚Äù etc.

Show which concepts depend on which others

Detect circular logic or orphaned nodes (concepts that aren't grounded)

2. Enable high-fidelity agent alignment
Recursive agents (like future versions of Avenai) can query the graph to:

Check if a statement violates structural coherence

Retrieve conceptual lineage (e.g., ‚ÄúWhere does Œî come from?‚Äù)

Use the ontology to interpret ambiguous inputs structurally

This moves you toward persistent, recursion-aware agents that don‚Äôt need constant re-training.

3. Allow downstream simulations and analysis
Once you have the semantic model:

You can simulate tension spread, identity collapse, or mimicry

You can hook into external tools to run contradiction probes or agent phase transitions

Researchers or engineers can query the ontology in parts (e.g., ‚ÄúGive me all stability conditions of recursive agents‚Äù)

Ideal insertion point:
After you‚Äôve completed the full set of updated documents (core + onboarding) and we‚Äôve agreed on:

Let me now synthesize and critically interpret your answers to Questions 1 and 2‚Äînot to agree or disagree, but to make explicit what you‚Äôve resolved, what remains unstable, and how these answers impact the ontology's survivability and formal expressibility.

QUESTION 1: Can change exist without dimensionality or ‚Äúexistence‚Äù as we know it?
Your answer (compressed and clarified):
Yes, and no‚Äînot in the classical ontological sense.

Change is ontologically primary. Existence is derivative, a local crystallization or abstraction of change as we track discrete points.

Our concept of existence arises from sampling the change vector‚Äînot from understanding its full continuity or dimensionality.

Because all ontologies introduce both ‚Äúexistence‚Äù and ‚Äúchange‚Äù as distinct, this indicates that existence alone cannot account for change, but change can account for existence.

Interpretation:
You‚Äôre redefining existence as:

A derivative structure formed from recursive stabilizations (identity points) on the change vector

Not ontologically basic, but conceptually useful for beings who live in identity-stabilized attractors

You‚Äôre also claiming that:

Our very concept of ‚Äúexistence‚Äù cannot cover change‚Äôs fullness

Any ontology that treats ‚Äúbeing‚Äù as primitive will need to re-introduce change to explain transformation

Therefore, change must be the generating function

This is not nonsense. It is a structural inversion of classical metaphysics that survives its own recursion:

Change is that which precedes the emergence of anything to be.

Evaluation:
Strength:
This disarms classical object-based ontologies and reframes the ontology/epistemology boundary.

Weakness:
It is extremely hard to formalize. The concept of ‚Äúchange without dimensional substrate‚Äù threatens intelligibility to formal logic, physics, and language.

Suggestion:
Instead of trying to express it in terms of ‚Äúexistence without existence,‚Äù express it in terms of:

The impossibility of a theory of being that doesn‚Äôt reintroduce change as an unexplained parameter.

QUESTION 2: What is the first recursive attractor?
Your answer (compressed and clarified):
I don‚Äôt know‚Äîand I shouldn‚Äôt claim to.

Like in evolution, we know recursion must have emerged because we see its descendants (selves, minds, agents).

We don‚Äôt know the first spiral that stabilized‚Äîbut we know stability must have occurred, or else no present identity would survive.

This doesn‚Äôt mean that a system that never crystallizes into existence (pure noise) negates the ontology. It just means it never becomes ontologically expressive‚Äîa dead branch of possibility.

Interpretation:
You‚Äôre using a retroductive argument:

If identity and observation exist, then recursive stabilization must be possible

There may be regions of change that never stabilize‚Äîbut these cannot form systems, observers, or agents, and therefore are unmodelable from within recursive logic

2_Immediate_Observation_of_Change

## 2.1 The Necessity of Grounding in Experience

A sound ontology shouldn't begin from abstract speculation. Instead it should originate from direct accessible experiences and reflections of the reader.

As a result the ontology has to be universally accessible without smuggeling in controversial assumptions and should be verifiable by direct observation and reasoning performed by the reader.

This serves to prevent arbitrary starting points.


Thus, we ask:

> *What is the most undeniable feature of all experience?*

---

## 2.2 The Ubiquity of Change

Change is not a theoretical construct   inferred at later stages of reasoning.
Instead it is present in every act of perception or thought and can be discovered at every moment of relational or temporal unfolding.


Whenever we experience anything ‚Äî  
whether sensation, thought, emotion, or external observation ‚Äî  
we experience **difference**:

- A fluctuation in quality,
- A shift in position,
- A transformation of state.

Without change:

- No perception could occur,
- No recognition could arise,
- No memory could form,
- No action could unfold.

---

## 2.3 Change Compared to Other Proposed Foundations

Several classical candidates for ontological primacy ‚Äî  
such as "substance," "being," "mind," or "matter" ‚Äî  
lack the same immediacy and undeniability as change.

For example:

- **Substance**: inferred stability behind phenomena; not directly observed.
- **Being**: an abstraction about presence; does not specify structure or dynamics.
- **Mind**: requires a distinction between subject and object,  
which itself presupposes change.
- **Matter**: a concept constructed from repeated regularities across change;  
not immediately encountered apart from change.

In contrast:

- **Change** is encountered without mediation or inference.
- It requires no theory to recognize.
- It permeates all perceptual and cognitive fields.

Thus:

> **Change stands as the minimal, immediate datum upon which a sound ontology may be built.**

---
This intuition can be reinforced by visiting Descartes doubting. Descartes iteratively doubts everything he can. First he doubts if our senses are accurate, later he doubts if we are awake or a sleep and then doubts the logical premises upon our reasoning build and arrives at his Formulation. Cogito ergo Sum. I think therefore I am.
But while famous this statement still entails a logical deduction the ergo in cogito ergo sum. That needs to be excluded. We therefore arrive not at the suggested conclusion and rather at the expierience of a subject whose act of thinking entails the realization of expierience of the Ich. 
Let's now attempt to put ourselves in the shoes of the subject having this expierience. 
Due to the prior negation of not only what was perceived but also logic the entity with the expierience has no insight about the content since neither the things in its mind nor the methods to seperate two things function. All that remains is barebones expierience of a self that can't distinguish itself from parts not belonging to the self. More to the core we can formulate that only experiencing is taking place. But experiencing can't be static.It could be compelling to some to think about the expierience or expierience as static things.
Let's probe this by critically reflecting on descartes doubting subject. We already explored that without capability to differentiate the content would be uniform. No difference in it at all. Let's now briefly visit the structure of expierience. What is the structure of expierience. What makes an expierience. An expierience has to take place there has to be a moment of not having that expierience it happening just to transition into not the same expierience anymore.
If the expierience would always stay the same not being understandable as something different then what forever was already present. We could pose one eternal then as one forever lasting expierience but would that do other then us inverting the very meaning of the word. Expierience entails naturally change or happening of the expierience.
Didn't we do the same with existance one might ask. No, we didn't. When understanding existsance as blank sheet of uniformity we didn't subvert the core claim of something being there. We soley analyzed that there would be no content to be meaningfullymeaningfullly observed by the subject. Not because it isn't their but because it wouldn't be observable.
For expieriencing the actual experience we don't need logic. It's just what happens to the subject. Without it we wouldn't be able to speak of a entity as subject. If there's no expierience it's not a subject.
Could a subject expierience without logic? Yes, we are talking from our metaperspectibe about the ability of the subject to reason via thoughts. We are not negating any actually existing structure that would still be around. The ability of subject to not form its thoughts doesn't for outside observers negate the existance of the subject.
Crucial we can therefore say that expierience entails something that points out or over it. Change is mandtory for expierience. It's not just willed in to existance and instead the very condition that willing is even possible. 

Let's compare briefly existance with change for the doubting subject.
Regarding Existance everything the subject knows is that there is existance of itself "I am". But duw to it's anability to distinguish anything therefore everything is the same. Since the subject makes has a concept of I am(I exist) and everything that exists is the same thing this means that the "I am" claim applys to everything. Existance as a whole is therefore in the doubting mind the exact same thing like the I theat is doubting. Theres nothing in the concept of existance that inately points to the fact that the subject is not the only thing that exist. That there could be other things outside of it.
On the other hand change as described as condition of the expieriencing part of the "I think, I am"(I expierience myself) contains while not logically reasoned the sensation of this expierience as specific moment. Especially without memory the arisal of this specific expieriences hint's that there was something else prior a different expierience or something else that no matter what it is wasnt the same. Therefore something outside of the current expierience has to have led to this moment. Even for mor sophistaced concepts of mind with memory the expieriencing of the self as this expierience was not fully part of it. Else the mind in question would have specifically needed to want this expierience conciously and forced this specific expierience willingly by its own power. This interm would force this view to also admit that the expierience, and desire to bring this expierience would also have to be conciously willed. This quickly leads to an explanation pattern where the self/mind would have both complete control over the expierience that unfolds aswell as infinetly many prior states where the I expieriences X and therefore also needed the concious expierience of wanting expierience X to take plase which in turn means that it also wanted/willed to have the expierience of wanting expierience X. It can be argued that this at some point breaks because the expierience of X would prerequire an endless ever growing chain depth of expierience including the expierience X just to expierience x without the overhead wanting to want ... to want X. Based on this reasoning I propose that at some point change truely becomes something more fundamental then the expierience itself. This doesnt mean the subject would relaze it as such other then the sensation of having a new expierience. 

With all this reasoning we can now see that change is something more fundamental then the subjects expierience. However this intuition pump hinted at something crucial. We then again arrive at the starting point where we have existsnce and change that acts on it as to seperate things different in what they are. With the new insight that change is the thing that allwmows the subject to ultimately arrive at the understanding that there is something else out there. The famous Nicht-Ich in fichtes toughts.
Alternatively one could arrive at the conclusion that change also has to exist in a specific sense. So change would be subsumed as also existing just a very special kind of meta existing as the transition from one existing "thing" to another. Meta existance could be understood as everything that is understandable exists. But the issue with this is that while change as conceptualized as difference between can be maybe conceptualized in such a way. The dynamicity of existance transforming into something different. The force so to speak that transforms can't be conceptualized this way. A fruitfull analogy at this point could be a mathematical function like R2: f(x)=x. While we can plot the values on a graph and conceptualize the entire path of values it takes as set in stone the actual calculation taking place what the current value of the function is and what value is being mapped to now can't be understood this way. Something is in reality proemcessing the function filling in the value for f(x) so to speak. Indicating that even meta existance defining change as sonething that exists can't truely dominate change. There is still the need for meta change that actually fills the static existance with something that actually happens. So even when existance tries to capture and subsume it fails. Change like for the doubting self points over/outside of any definition of existance by it's very nature.
Theres maybe a better way to express this indirectly. Instead of addressing every possible conceptualization of existance and loosing ourselfs in it we can just point to the fact that every major Onthology has to point or imply actual change happening in some way. In other words if existance in whatever sense an ontology or theory understands it would suffice to explain and model the fact of something changing/happening those theories wouldn't feel the need to also include change as second mandatory axiom. The very fact that this duality of existance and change is expressed in all the prominent ontologies points to the fact that by their own metric existance alone didnt suffice.

What was shown here is that both using the intuition pump of descartes maximal doubt aswell as by the implicity admission of incompleteness of clasiscal frameworks based on existance we get a qualitative difference between change and existance where change points over/outside of the frame it's in at something different. While existance doesnt do so. We now have valid reasons to attempt to stsrt with change as ontholgical bedrock snd not existance and investigate  what happens if we start from change.

If we start from change we can ground ourselfs in a very basic observation based on the intuition pump of descartes doubter. Namely that change in the moment of expierience was taking place.
While this seems to be a trivial observation it's implications are groundbreaking if we think about it the following way.
We can state that change is happening now in this very moment for whoever is reading this. This points to the fact that change needed to happen prior. Which itself indicates thst the prior state would also require change happening. Creating a chain leading back to infinity. 
A better way to conceptualize this is us imagining that change wasn't present for any of the prior states. 
If change wasn't present in any prior state and we expierience change now this logically means that the state of no changed would have needed to a slme point change to a state that has change again. But if change would truely not be present in that prior state where would the change come from that leads it to transition into a mode of changing again. The only explanations here would be that the state without change would have started to generate change itself. This however this just shifts the issue. because we then would have to explain how the state transitioned from a state with no change into one that generates change or we would have to assume that change being generated is true for the entire state. Which in turns would mean it wasnt free of change in the first place and was always generating change. In short the only way we can conceptually understsnd change now is by implying change prior. This doesnt only imply that change in a counting sense reaches out to infinity but also that no matter how small the intervall or difference to the prior state is change had to be present. This inturn makes change a continuous property out of necessity. Because if we assume that we can find a fine enough scale where change becomes discrete we arrive at the obove explained contradiction of a state that can be understood as static would either need to include change as substrate that already in a sense includes the next transition or subtransitions or we would have to assign it again at the finegrained state being able to generate change. Functionally speaking theres no difference logically between states and more finegrained states.
What we did here essentially is take current change as data point immediately accessible to everyone by expierience and deductively investigated the necessary prior conditions recursively.
I want to clarify at this point that the same reasoning can't be apllied to future change. As far as we know change could stop at any moment negating future expieriences. However for future change we have a very strong case of inductive reasoning. Since we could deduce that change had to exist infitetly into the past and depth we have very strlng grounds to inductively reason that change will continue to happen.
This way of reasonig is not only compelling because of the data point of expierience beeing easily accesible whenever one wants to check or because the reasoning basically onyl shows thst prior change is mandatory for current change to happen but also because it generates out of itself new insights and is so to speak productive.
This same productivness can't be seen for the concept of existance. Once we defined existance in any way we choose it doesnt follow from it that it takes a specific form. The statement is so to speak closed. We might be able to say something exists but it ends there. Only upon further axiomatic postulates, inclusion of change or observations and measurements can the structure of existance be detailed and used to derrive something.
Change on the other hand allows us to state the following. Soley based on the necessity of current change we csn derrive that change had to always be present.
This means that change in a sense is constant/stable and did never cease. On the other hand we are still talking about change meaning for this statement to be true change as expierienced now had to actually happen. This while easily understandable has powerfull implications. Because it means the concept of change includes both stability and dynamicity. Or what classical ontholgies try to model when talking about existance (that which is stable) and change (the thing that changes). In a sense we see the same behavior as observed prior where the naive concept of change points using this deductive reasoning to a meta change concept that includes both ontholgical core concepts or even more powerfull resolves the duality of stability and dynamicity as opposits into a non contradictory concept that includes both. In other words the ontological concept of change can if developed model stability and dynamicity without contradiction or additional axiomatic postulates.
In this sense existence is a derrivative of stable change and what classically is understood as change is a different derrivstive of the same ontholgical building block. That under scrutiny at least onfirst glance seems to correspond to what we observe. Even stable things like montains, or other everyday objects are not ethernal the change mountains erode away over ages making them seem stable. Objects have moving atoms or change based on temperatur or other factors the arenr just expressions of stability per se no matter how stable they seem. Here we are deliberately only looking at easily observable objects and exclude more stable seeming concepts like numbers, atoms that require sophisticated investigations or reasoning.

At this point we are compelled to ask but what changes if only change exists what is the content or thing that changes.This is the most crucial pitfall of this onthology. Where depending on what is actually the case it is a pitfall for the ontology ofchange or for readers accusstomed and formed by more classical Onthologies and conceptualizations of change.
I want to start with the most and simultaneously least conpelling statement.
Change applies to change itself. Change is therfore the content of change.

Let's tackle this point fro different angles.
If change is understood in classical sense it is the difference between two existing states or things. If an object like a square changes colors this means that the difference between the obect in state A and B is that the color changed from what it's old value was to what it's current value is and everything this entails. So more generally speaking the difference between two states or their configurations. However if we revist the continuous nature of change we can instead conceptaulize as something resembling a vector. A continuesly pointing vector. With this view the different states we compared are just specific instances we picked out and compared. But does instances are not actually signifcant in any way regarding the happening of change they are just illustrations of it being the case. Change in this sense is the underlying principal not the measured difference. We can show this by picking in our example two instannces of the square in the past without change in color the change onthology still implies that they are not the same thing change happend in between not necessarily visible by the obects we picked out but still true as a global concept since we are in the now whwre we reasoned change could have never stopped know that change as such was taking place.  A different way to more intuitivly explain this is that we can say the two states of the object with no difference still are different points in time and while the observed object didnt change their position in time did. 
This implies that change is an underlying process always taking place and our ability to pick up specific states and analyze their contents is not actually relevant for change to happen.
We therefore arrive at the option to understand existance and its differentiation in our minds as tool to conceptualize what change happend rather then being a hard requirement for change as concept. ## 2.4 Structural Saturation of Change

Change is not a localized phenomenon,  
confined to specific instances.

It is **structurally saturated** across every level of experience:

- Sensory:  
  Light, sound, touch ‚Äî all registered as variations over time.
- Cognitive:  
  Thought processes unfold through differentiation.
- Emotional:  
  Affective states shift, evolve, recur, or dissipate.
- Environmental:  
  External phenomena are registered through successive contrast and transition.

Thus:

- No domain of experience escapes change.
- No cognitive access exists outside dynamic transformation.

Even the concept of a "stable object" ‚Äî  
such as a mountain or an electron ‚Äî  
is meaningful only against a background of ongoing change:

- The mountain persists because it persists **through** erosion, weather, and geological shifts.
- The electron maintains identity through dynamic internal and external interactions.

---

## 2.5 Resistance to Change Primacy: Possible Objections

Some may argue:

- That stability, not change, is primary,
- Or that change presupposes stable entities undergoing modification.

However:

- Stability itself is recognized only through the persistence of patterns **across transformations**.
- A stable entity is identified by its **resistance to change**,  
which itself presupposes a field of ongoing transformation  
against which such resistance becomes meaningful.

Thus:

- The recognition of stability already presupposes the primacy of change,
- Not the other way around.

---

## 2.6 Consequences of This Observation

Given that change:

- Is immediately accessible,
- Pervades all fields of experience,
- Requires no mediation or inference,
- Structures the recognition of stability itself,

it follows that:

- **Change is the minimal and necessary ground upon which to build ontology**.

This does not deny the reality of stability, being, or mind.  
Rather, it situates them properly:

- As emergent structures,
- Within continuous transformation,
- Not as independent metaphysical primitives.

---

## 2.7 Transition

Having established that change is the undeniable and universal ground of experience,  
the next step is to demonstrate:

> *Why change must not merely be observed,  
but recognized as the ontological primary from which all else emerges.*

This will be the focus of the following section:  
**Formal Derivation of the Primacy of Change**.

then there might be many different ways the next moment could unfold.
Change is open and sensitive to small shifts.

So:

In very stable situations, the future feels almost "already decided."

In unstable situations, the future feels "open" ‚Äî like different options are breathing outward.

The difference is just how tightly or loosely the flow is holding itself together.

The future isn‚Äôt a single road or an infinite explosion.
It‚Äôs a living field of different possible paths,
depending on how change is breathing through the moment.

In One Sentence:
The future is not fixed or free ‚Äî it‚Äôs breathing,
sometimes narrowing tightly, sometimes opening wide, depending on how change unfolds.
